  30. I compared this Sonicare 6731 to the 6921 (see my review of that product as well for a comparison to the Oral-B product). The 6731 has the exact same cleaning speed and can utilize the same brushhead (in fact, these models can use the DiamondClean Brush head that comes with the most expensive Sonicare model, should that be your desire). What's missing....you still get the travel case, you still get only one brush head (the ProResults model), and you get the same cleaning ability. So the differences: the induction charger base for the 6731 is smaller and does not hold spare brush heads. I view this as a positive since it takes less counter space. The unit itself does not have quite the range of modes as the 6921. It doesn't, for example, have the capability of cleaning at high speed for 2 minutes and then switch to a gum cleaning mode for 1 minute. It does, however, have a 30 second polish addition capability. Frankly, I suspect most people just turn these brushes on, brush their teeth using whatever mode they always use, turn the brush off and call it a day. Sort of like washing machines and dishwashers...if you find yourself using all the modes those products offer, maybe you want the 6921 or better. But if not, save a few bucks and get the 6731. It brushes exceptionally well and your teeth will be clean.
  31. I have been using it for the past month as a replacement from my old electric toothbrush that finally died (the batteries in these things seem to only be good for 2-3 years and are never replaceable).<br /><br />The old one had the larger diameter handle and brushes with a large screw on base and a magnetic induction coupling to the motor. The problem with that style is that there's a large volume of space under the base of the brush that fills up with disgusting bacterial slime.<br /><br />So I really like the small push-on brush that this one uses, and the slim handle is lighter and more comfortable to hold.<br /><br />The charging system is also well done in that you don't just leave the brush on the charger stand all the time (although you can). The battery light on the handle will start to blink after a week or so of use and you can then charge it overnight and go for another week without worrying about it. This means the battery is not constantly charging and will probably last a lot longer as a result.<br /><br />I don't see too much value in the extra \\"modes\\" this brush offers. Sure if you want a more gentle option that's there, but the whitening cycle which switches to an extra 20 seconds of a different vibration pattern at the end of the regular brushing cycle seems a bit of a gimmick, but then I can't claim to have used it enough to know and maybe it's just awesome for whitening your teeth.<br /><br />Because of the different modes (accessed by repeatedly pressing the single button) if you want to turn the brush off in the middle of a cycle you need to hold the button down for couple seconds. This is actually more annoying than just pressing it a couple times to cycle through the modes back to off which is what I usually do. So the user interface isn't all that great, but what can you do with just one button? There are at least separate green LEDs to tell you what mode it's in.<br /><br />Anyhow, I have so far found nothing else to complain about, and would have no reservations recommending it.<br /><br />G.

  51. The Phillips Sonicare Healthywhite is designed to make your teeth whiter in 2 weeks. My teeth get good care, having used first another model Sonicare and then and Braun electric toothbrush for years. So coming into this, I have pretty decent teeth care. The question is: does this thing really make your teeth whiter? So, I took a picture of my teeth before and after a two-week period of brushing with the Healthywhite Sonicare. I then took each image, placed them in a graphic-editing program (Adobe Fireworks) and compared the color values. The hexadecimal values use a range from #FFFFFF (pure white) to #000000 (pure black.) The higher the values, the lighter the images. Here are the results:<br /><br />Time 1: #A17962 (10582370) [hex/decimal]<br />Time 2: #F8F8F8 (16316664) [hex/decimal]<br /><br />The difference between the values is about 54%. That is, I found a 54% brighter image (higher hex value) after brushing with the Sonicare brush for two weeks. I cannot guarantee that the lighting was exactly right or anything else was the same other than I used the same iPhone camera to get the digital images used with my graphic editing tool. However, even allowing for the differences in lighting, I'm pretty sure that it did get my teeth whiter just from looking at them. (It's not as easy as you think to judge the whiteness of your teeth after two a two-week comparison.)<br /><br />The protocol for brushing your teeth is a bit more complex. Each quadrant in your mouth is given a value of 1 to 4. (Outside teeth are odd values; inside, even.) The process has six steps: Q1, Q2, Q3, Q4 and then back to Q1 and Q2 for a second time when using the special white teeth mode. When I first read the instructions, it seemed fairly complex for just brushing my teeth, but the brush signals when it's time to change quadrants and it wasn't too difficult to get used to.<br /><br />Two issues I found with the brush I believe could use improvement. First, the brush has three modes, and to change from one mode to the next, you click a power button--one, two or three clicks. The whitener mode is three clicks. The first time I did this, I put on toothpaste on the brush, and since I had to look at a green light that indicates which of the three modes I was in, I held it out to where I could see it. After cycling through the first two modes, the toothpaste was off my brush and all over me and the bathroom mirror. (If you're thinking, \\"Why not wait until you're in the right mode to put on the toothpaste?\\"; you try it with the thing vibrating like a nervous Chihuahua.) If you first are able set the mode, and then turn it on, you could avoid this problem. What good are those green mode-indicator lights going to do if the brush is in your mouth? I found that by putting the brush in my mouth before starting it and then pressing the mode button three times, I avoided this problem. Second, the stand is very small and easy to knock over (as I did as soon as I set it up.) The small stand is great for travel, but pretty unstable otherwise.<br /><br />Overall, I really like this brush and will continue to use it, blinding the world with white, bright teeth.hange from one mode to the next, you click a power button--one, two or three clicks. The whitener mode is three clicks. The first time I did this, I put on toothpaste on the brush, and since I had to look at a green light that indicates which of the three modes I was in, I held it out to where I could see it. After cycling through the first two modes, the toothpaste was off my brush and all over me and the bathroom mirror. (If you're thinking, \\"Why not wait until you're in the right mode to put on the toothpaste?\\"; you try it with the thing vibrating like a nervous Chihuahua.) If you first are able set the mode, and then turn it on, you could avoid this problem. What good are those green mode-indicator lights going to do if the brush is in your mouth? I found that by putting the brush in my mouth before starting it and then pressing the mode button three times, I avoided this problem. Second, the stand is very small and easy to knock over (as I did as soon as I set it up.) The small stand is great for travel, but pretty unstable otherwise.<br /><br />Overall, I really like this brush and will continue to use it, blinding the world with white, bright teeth.
  52. I haven't had a Philips Sonicare toothbrush in YEARS; mine was an early model that took what are now being sold as e-Series replacement heads. When it died, I migrated to the Braun model that my dentist sold at his cost and which I never liked quite as much. When it died, I went back to manual brushing but have kept an eye on both models in the ensuing years. Now I'm back to Sonicare, an updated model, and I couldn't be happier.<br /><br />I believe the Philips Sonicare HX6731/O2 HealthyWhite Rechargeable (*whew*) is a newer model; however, it's kind of difficult to determine with this line. Regardless, this is by no means their top-of-the-line model (I think that distinction is held by the DiamondClean). However, it's the more deluxe of their two-model HealthyWhite line which promises its users whiter teeth (two shades!) in two weeks if used in \\"Clean and White\\" mode; this adds another, gentler 15 seconds to the teeth in each front quadrant. I don't think this worked for me but then, my teeth are pretty much impervious to any but clinical whitening efforts and even those don't last (thanks, tetracyclene) so I'm not the ideal person to review this function. However, my teeth haven't felt this clean after brushing in years, and for that I'm thankful.<br /><br />This particular HealthyWhite model has three cycles--the aforementioned \\"Clean and White,\\" \\"Clean\\" (sans \\"and White\\"), and \\"Sensitive\\" (which I haven't used). It's a good idea to start with \\"Clean\\" because it gradually and unobtrusively eases one into the the full-strength function over the course of the first 14 brushings. Cleaning is amazing. Rather than rotating, Sonicare brushes tend to blast whatever shouldn't be there off your teeth and gums. New users might find the experience a little weird (I didn't) but I recommend sticking with it because the rewards are potentially great. The cycles are a good way of segueing into this model's few cons.<br /><br />One is required to choose the cycle AFTER turning on the brush by means of continuing to push the \\"On\\" button. This, frankly, is something of a pain because one then has to wonder if the intended cycle is actually engaged (unless you want to try to study yourself in the mirror). It's also necessary for the brush to be in use in order to determine how much charge it's holding. And a \\"pause\\" feature would also be nice for those times when ones dentition-related ablutions are interrupted for some reason or other. In addition, this particular model comes with only one brush--the standard size. At this price point, including a brush with a smaller head would give new owners an idea of which brush best fits their needs, especially if the default standard brush is larger than is optimal. (My frustrated dentist trying to work in my rather small mouth: \\"Remember when your mother used to tell you that you have a big mouth? She was lying.\\") And the replacement brushes are by no means cheap, especially if you want to upgrade to the DiamondClean line.<br /><br />I charged my Philips Sonicare HX6731/O2 HealthyWhite Rechargeable for a full 24 hours prior to first using it and don't leave it in its charger between uses, even though the instructions state that I can. I find that the charge holds a good two weeks, which is good for short trips, and intend to allow it to fully discharge before charging it for another 24 hours. A nice travel case is included, as well as a protective cap for the brush head. A stand to hold the brush head would have been nice; I stand mine upright in the medicine chest after use in order to let it air dry.<br /><br />I've read reviews of other Sonicare models that state there have been quality control problems that result breakage. I guess time will tell and I'll update this review if I experience problems. Registering the HealthyWhite Rechargeable online extends the 2-year warranty by another six months; the downside is that one is required to open an online Philips account in order to do this (for my money, a PITA). Bear in mind that a solid warranty is a good thing only if it guarantees your product on the offhand chance that there's a problem; repeated warranty claims, even if honored, come down to a product one can't depend upon and that's not in your possession, doing its intended job. I'll keep my fingers crossed.<br /><br />All in all, I'm crazy about the HealthyWhite Rechargeable and so far am delighted with it. Five stars for the time being.

At first I thought this was some kind of design bug but now I wonder if it's done to give the user an indication of how much longer it would brush. The replacement brushes for the Essence are a significant amount of the unit including metal and plastic. On Amazon a two pack will run you $25. The replacement brushes for the HX6731 are just about the bare minimum of material which seems to me to make a lot more sense.   70. My viewpoint is that of a first-time electric toothbrush user. Making a switch from a standard toothbrush to an electric is not really something we think about regularly. Perhaps it is psychological -- we are so accustomed to standard toothbrushes that electrics just seem odd and out of place even if you have regular dentist appointments.<br /><br />So the key to making a permanent switch is making it as easy and understandable as possible, highlighting the advantages. Philips seems to me to have successfully done this with this Sonicare HX6731 product. For one thing, the manual is incredibly easy to understand. From the beginning, it explains that the basic function is to keep you on track evenly brushing your teeth for exactly two minutes, which is what dentists recommend. One button push and you're started. Every 30 seconds, the unit slightly beeps at you to let you know to move on the next section of your teeth (back and front of upper teeth, back and front of lower teeth). If you have sensitive teeth, there is a special setting for you too.<br /><br />The build of the device is quite attractive. It cleans very easily with water. The top bristles section is EASILY removable so that you can replace it quickly, or I suppose even share the motorized part of the unit with someone else (i.e. that someone else has an additional top bristles section just for them). It's recommended that you replace the bristle part every few months.<br /><br />Another nice feature is actually the charging. I don't know about you, but when I clean my electric razor with water, I get a little nervous about plugging it into the recharger. This electric toothbrush does NOT have a similar issue. Basically, from what I can tell it uses an inductive charging method which requires no connected wires (it's complicated, don't ask me to explain). At any rate, there's no way you can get shocked while recharging. Nifty!<br /><br />Last little tip... I recommend that you put it in your mouth *before* turning it on to brush. That way, as it begins to vibrate it doesn't shower water droplets everywhere and thus annoy you.* turning it on to brush. That way, as it begins to vibrate it doesn't shower water droplets everywhere and thus annoy you.

They change between `teeth whitening mode', normal clean, and `sensitive'. In teeth whitening mode, for example, it expects you to brush for 2 minutes where you alternate sides of your teeth for 30 seconds a piece (they label your mouth as having 4 sections). After the 2 minute mark it will turn into the teeth whitening function.<br /><br />The latter is a little to mechanical and procedural for me and thus I ended up using it as normal with it turned on. **For this it actually did whiten the teeth a bit more so than using the manual toothbrush. **<br /><br />[---Final Thoughts---]<br /><br />A little more expensive then I feel it is worth however it is a solidly packed, small-sized, tooth brush that is very portable. It does well at `precision' cleaning as compared to manually using a toothbrush because it loosens any particles on your teeth better than doing it by hand. For better cleaning, this electric toothbrush will be the choice for you. Just know you will giggle the first few times you use it.l giggle the first few times you use it.

  365. Our dentist recommended SonicCare to my wife and me and we finally caved in and got one. The Healthy White was the model I purchased first, because it looked like it had more \\"features.\\" The brush works great and my wife and I are sold on the concept now. After using SonicCare for a couple of weeks, we both feel that our teeth are, in fact, much cleaner, that our gums are getting better treatment, too. But I think the Healthy White model was a mistake. The much cheaper Easy Clean model has just one mode -- \\"Clean\\" -- and that is all you need. Clean mode both on the Easy Clean and the Healthy White (and presumably on other models) gives you the 2-minute timer that beeps every 30 seconds to tell you to move to a different quadrant of your mouth. That's excellent -- and it's all you need. The extra features on the more expensive Healthy White are unnecessary. The \\"sensitive\\" mode is (in my opinion) utterly useless. The normal \\"clean\\" mode is already pretty delicate. And the \\"clean and white\\" mode on this model is also unnecessary. All it does it add 30 seconds to the timer. If you want to brush your teeth for more than 2 minutes using the less expensive one-mode Easy Clean model, just turn the brush back on (tap the power button) and brush away for 30 more seconds or however long you like. The Healthy White model has no other advantages. So, four stars because I highly recommend the SoniCare system. If you persuade yourself that you really really need those extra features, God bless you. But the one I recommend is the less expensive Easy Clean model. Addendum: Note that the brush heads have smooth 'necks' as a consequence of which it can be a bit tricky to remove the head, especially if the brush is wet and a bit sudsy when you're done brushing. I ended up buying a second brush (the Easy Clean model) so my wife and I wouldn't have to struggle to pull the head off constantly. I think SoniCare should add a couple gripping ridges to the neck of the brush head --but I'm not taking any points off for that small design flaw.

So the toothbrush has to be turned on -- meaning vibrating at a high rate of speed -- to change the mode. The default is &#34;Clean.&#34; But of course, if you're buying this toothbrush, you probably want to use the &#34;Clean and White&#34; function. You have to hit the power button twice more to toggle it over to &#34;Clean and White,&#34; while the violent vibrations fling your toothpaste into the sink. You might think you can do that while the toothbrush is in your mouth, but then you can't see the indicator light that tells you what mode you're in.<br /><br />It's also much louder than my old FlexCare was and once I've got it into &#34;Clean and White&#34; mode, I don't like the whitening function. It's got a different vibration and it makes my mouth feel funny. Also, to be clear, to use the &#34;Clean and White&#34; function, you're going to have to brush your teeth for three minutes, not two. I bet you could just use the extra long cleaning cycle on the FlexCare and get the same results. (My guess is the weird harder vibration is mostly to make you feel like it's doing something different/extra, but it actually works by forcing you to brush longer.)<br /><br />If I'd known I was going to end up using this only in &#34;clean&#34; mode, I would've bought the most basic one at half the price. Alternatively, I could have sprung for a more expensive one where I could change modes easily. But this is the worst of both worlds -- higher price, but the extras aren't functional.<br /><br />I should add, by the way, that if you're on the fence about whether to buy a Sonicare at all, I'm a big fan of the brand. When I travel without it,I really notice the difference. I just don't like this particular model.
